    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="91885bc498e34142af1709b5ce1eb94e">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '5926d3f7-a73f-4d74-b95e-0628e244c46a'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d151145717" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>Iodized polyacrylonitrile anodes (I-PAN) can regulate the bulk/interphase metal-ion diffusion kinetics and metal-ion desolvation chemistry of the electrolyte, and simultaneously reutilize the dead/plated metal. Benefiting from these features, the unprecedented extremely fast-charging performance can be achieved for a series of metal (Li, Na)-ion batteries using I-PAN as anodes.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/65a8797d-3e4c-4d4d-8dc0-c47713f50d3b/anie202315564-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/65a8797d-3e4c-4d4d-8dc0-c47713f50d3b/anie202315564-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/77b8c265-a2f4-4bf1-a31b-09fd35a275f5/anie202315564-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/65a8797d-3e4c-4d4d-8dc0-c47713f50d3b/anie202315564-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d151145756" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The fast-charging capability of rechargeable batteries is greatly limited by the sluggish ion transport kinetics in anode materials. Here we develop an iodized polyacrylonitrile (I-PAN) anode that can boost the bulk/interphase lithium (Li)-ion diffusion kinetics and accelerate Li-ion desolvation process to realize high-performance fast-charging Li-ion batteries. The iodine immobilized in I-PAN framework expands ion transport channels, compresses the electric double layer, and changes the inner Helmholtz plane to form LiF/LiI-rich solid electrolyte interphase layer. The dissolved iodine ions in the electrolyte self-induced by the interfacial nucleophilic substitution of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> not only promote the Li-ion desolvation process, but also reuse the plated/dead Li formed on the anode under fast-charging conditions. Consequently, the I-PAN anode exhibits a high capacity of 228.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (39 % of capacity at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> delivered in 18 seconds) and negligible capacity decay for 10000 cycles at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The I-PAN||LiNi<sub>0.8</sub>Co<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> full cell shows excellent fast-charging performance with attractive capacities and negligible capacity decay for 1000 cycles at extremely high rates of 5 C and 10 C (1 C=180 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). We also demonstrate high-performance fast-charging sodium-ion batteries using I-PAN anodes.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202315564-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202315564-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Fast charging, as one of the key technologies of rechargeable batteries, has drawn extensive interests recently and shown a great market prospect.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0001_R_d151145617e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> The strategic goal of fast-charging aims to recharge 80 % of the state-of-charge for batteries in 15 min (fast-charging, 4 C), and more preferably within 10 min (extremely fast-charging, 6 C).<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0004_R_d151145617e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Currently, the successful realization of fast-charging capability is hindered by the limited mass transport of the battery materials in the battery system.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0007_R_d151145617e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> When batteries are charged at high rates, large polarizations mainly originated from the sluggish mass transport kinetics will result in serious metal plating and parasitic reaction of the electrolyte, which cause the accelerated performance degradation and safety risks.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0010_R_d151145617e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Under normal circumstances, the migration of metal ions in the batteries during the charging process involves the steps of metal ions extracting from the cathodes, diffusing in the electrolytes, passing through the electrode/electrolyte interphases, and intercalating in the anodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0011_R_d151145617e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> Among these steps, the metal-ion desolvation and ion diffusion in the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layers and anode materials are the main energy-consuming processes (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0001">1a</a>), which contribute significantly to the sluggish mass transport kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0014_R_d151145617e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> In view of this revelation, the strategies of tailoring ideal metal-ion solvation structure,<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0018_R_d151145617e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Energy">7</a></sup></span> interphase,<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0019_R_d151145617e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> and ion transport channels in anode materials<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0023_R_d151145617e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a></sup></span> are widely adopted to reduce the energy barrier of metal-ion transport. Especially for lithium (Li)-ion batteries, the electrolyte engineering is an effective method to regulate the Li-ion solvation structure. Recently, a number of novel electrolytes with weakened solvation structures have been developed to accelerate the Li-ion desolvation process, and meanwhile tune the SEI compositions and properties.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0024_R_d151145617e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> These electrolytes are beneficial for the improvement of the fast-charging performance to some extent, although the Li-ion conductivities of some electrolytes are relatively reduced.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0027_R_d151145617e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span> The design of advanced anode materials with fast ion transport channels is a direct way to lower the energy barrier of Li-ion solid-state diffusion. The reported anode materials with unique structures and Li-ion transport pathways, such as advanced carbon materials,<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0030_R_d151145617e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> niobium tungsten oxides,<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0033_R_d151145617e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nature">13</a></sup></span> disordered rock salt Li<sub>3+x</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0034_R_d151145617e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nature">14</a></sup></span> and niobium oxide,<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0035_R_d151145617e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Mater.">15</a></sup></span> show boosted Li-ion diffusion kinetics and good cycling performance at high rates. Despite the progress that has been made for the anode materials development, the majority of the researches are only focused on the regulation of Li-ion transport behavior within the bulk anodes and overlook the design of unique surface properties. The structures and functional groups on the surface of anode materials not only determine their interactions with Li ions and solvents to influence the Li-ion solvation structures, but also guide the fabrication of the electric double layer (EDL) covered on the anode, which have great effects on the ion desolvation process and SEI formation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0014_R_d151145617e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> Current anode materials without special surface functional groups lack the ability to promote Li-ion desolvation and interfacial ion diffusion, which still needs to be complemented by electrolyte engineering to meet the fast-charging requirements of practical applications. Thus, rationally constructing advanced anode materials with both fast ion-transport channels and optimized surface properties is highly desirable to simultaneously reduce the energy barriers of all the main energy-consuming processes of Li-ion transport to achieve high fast-charging performance.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202315564-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/90ba2d4c-ba97-4b0f-b704-17bd15029a83/anie202315564-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/90ba2d4c-ba97-4b0f-b704-17bd15029a83/anie202315564-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2218acf5-5084-4be2-ae85-543d34223872/anie202315564-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/90ba2d4c-ba97-4b0f-b704-17bd15029a83/anie202315564-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202315564-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202315564" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Schematic illustration of the multiple roles of I-PAN anodes. a, Energy-consuming processes of metal-ion transport in batteries. b, The I-PAN simultaneously regulates the bulk/interphase Li-ion diffusion kinetics, IHP and EDL structure, Li-ion desolvation chemistry of electrolyte, and plated/dead Li reutilization for fast-charging Li-ion batteries.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Herein, we report an iodized polyacrylonitrile (I-PAN) anode that can simultaneously regulate the bulk/interphase Li-ion diffusion kinetics and Li-ion desolvation chemistry of the electrolyte to realize high-performance fast-charging Li-ion batteries (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0001">1b</a>). C−I bonds in I-PAN significantly expand the ion transport channels to effectively enhance Li-ion solid-state diffusion kinetics and Li-ion storage of the I-PAN anodes. As an electron-rich and good leaving group, iodine on the surface of I-PAN can adsorb and concentrate Li ions, meanwhile suffers from the nucleophilic attack of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> to dissolve in the electrolyte, which changes the inner Helmholtz plane (IHP) and EDL structure on the I-PAN surface and enables the formation of LiF/LiI-rich SEI layer. Moreover, the dissolved iodine ions in the electrolyte can not only regulate the Li-ion solvation structure and promote the Li-ion desolvation process, but also reuse the plated/dead Li possibly formed on the anode to compensate the Li loss under extremely fast-charging conditions. As a result, the I-PAN anode exhibits an exceptional fast-charging performance, showing high capacities (325.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 228.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to 56 % and 39 % of the capacity at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> delivered in 35 and 18 seconds, respectively) and negligible capacity decay for 3000 cycles and 10000 cycles at extremely high current densities of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The I-PAN||LiNi<sub>0.8</sub>Co<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCM811) full cell delivers an attracting capacity of 156.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a capacity retention of 85.1 % after 1000 cycles at 5 C (1 C=180 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). Even under an extremely fast-charging condition of 10 C, an excellent cycling performance with a high capacity (112.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and capacity retention (91.4 % after 1000 cycles) is also achieved. Moreover, the I-PAN also shows great promise in fast-charging sodium (Na)-ion batteries, with a reversible capacity of 210 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (96.7 % capacity retention after 2000 cycles) and 186.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (87.2 % capacity retention after 3000 cycles) at 1 and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202315564-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202315564-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>I-PAN was synthesized through an iodine-assisted dehydrogenation polymerization method at a low thermal-pyrolysis temperature (Figure S1). Iodine was used as a dehydrogenation reagent for the polymerization of the polyacrylonitrile (PAN) and uniformly immobilized in the I-PAN framework in the form of C−I bonds. As a comparison, polymerized PAN (p-PAN) was prepared through the direct polymerization of PAN reactant at a high temperature. As shown in scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images, I-PAN displays a morphology of circular-shape particle with a diameter of around 100 nm and uniformly distributed C, N and I elements (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0002">2a</a>–<a href="#anie202315564-fig-0002">c</a>, Figure S2), implying iodine is homogeneously incorporated into the I-PAN framework. The powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) results demonstrate a broad diffraction peak at near 25° for both I-PAN and p-PAN (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0002">2d</a>), which is contributed to the characteristic (002) plane of the graphite/π-stacking conjugative carbon.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0036_R_d151145617e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16">16</a></sup></span> Interestingly, the (002) plane of I-PAN shifts to a lower angle compared with that of p-PAN, suggesting the enlarged interlayer distance enabled by the iodination.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0039_R_d151145617e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Commun.">17</a></sup></span> Moreover, no characteristic diffraction peaks of crystalline I<sub>2</sub> are detected in I-PAN (Figure S3), indicating the complete consumption of I<sub>2</sub> to form C−I bond. Raman spectrum of I-PAN shows two distinct peaks at around 1345 and 1585 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which can be assigned to the D band and G band, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0002">2e</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0040_R_d151145617e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon">18</a></sup></span> The intensity ratio (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) of D to G band increases from 1.29 (p-PAN) to 1.36 (I-PAN), revealing the iodine-assisted dehydrogenation polymerization changes the skeleton arrangement of the polymerized PAN framework. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) measurements were conducted to further investigate the compositions of I-PAN. The I 3d XPS spectrum of I-PAN displays two peaks at 630.1 eV (I 3d<sub>3/2</sub>) and 618.5 eV (I 3d<sub>5/2</sub>), assigned to C−I bond in the I-PAN backbones (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0002">2f</a>),<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0041_R_d151145617e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19">19</a></sup></span> which can be further confirmed by the presence of C−I peak at 285.9 eV in C 1s XPS spectrum (Figure S4).<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0044_R_d151145617e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span> The quantitative analysis of the XPS spectra shows that the iodine content in I-PAN is around 3.8 wt %. Compared with p-PAN, I-PAN shows a higher content of C−N=C group due to the enhanced polymerization degree enabled by the iodine-assisted dehydrogenation polymerization (Figure S4–5), which can be further evidenced by the higher N content (18.1 wt %) and lower H content (2.9 wt %) measured through the combustion method (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0002">2g</a>). The complete polymerization and iodination of PAN can also be verified by the <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectrum of I-PAN, which shows disappeared −C≡N group and clear C=C, C=N and C−I peaks at 117, 135 and 151 ppm, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0002">2h</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0045_R_d151145617e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Energy Lett.">21</a></sup></span> The density functional theory (DFT) calculation was conducted to study the molecular and electronic structures of p-PAN and I-PAN. The surface electrostatic potential (SESP) of p-PAN demonstrates a high polarity and uneven distribution of charges, due to the strong electron withdrawing effect of −C=N− group (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0002">2i</a>). By contrast, the I-PAN shows a more uniform SESP distribution and lower HOMO–LUMO band gap (0.0645 eV) than p-PAN owing to the presence of C−I bond, which facilitates the electron transfer in I-PAN (Figure S6).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202315564-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cf7b3ebf-02fd-44eb-ae1c-cd5dc29539f5/anie202315564-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cf7b3ebf-02fd-44eb-ae1c-cd5dc29539f5/anie202315564-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/882c3352-6ac5-4262-b506-7a3d245de40c/anie202315564-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cf7b3ebf-02fd-44eb-ae1c-cd5dc29539f5/anie202315564-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202315564-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202315564" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Characterization of I-PAN. a, b, TEM and HR-TEM images of I-PAN. c, STEM elemental mapping images of I-PAN. d, XRD patterns. e, Raman spectra. f, I 3d XPS spectrum of I-PAN. g, Elemental contents of the materials. h, <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectra of materials. i, SESP results of I-PAN and p-PAN.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>As an electron-rich and good leaving group, the existence of iodine significantly changes the surface properties of I-PAN. Compared with p-PAN (≈18 mV), the electron-rich iodine obviously enhances the surface electron density of I-PAN (≈48 mV), as verified by the surface potential measured with Kelvin probe force microscopy (KPFM) (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0003">3a</a>). The high surface electron density can enable strong Li-ion affinity and self-concentrate the Li ions on the surface of I-PAN. The DFT calculation results theoretically confirm the strong interactions between I-PAN and Li ions, showing a binding energy of −0.81 V. The optimized binding geometries clearly show that the electron-rich iodine can preferentially capture Li ions to form Li−I intermediate bond. In contrast, the binding energy between p-PAN and Li ions is +0.23 eV, indicating very weak Li-ion affinity of p-PAN (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0003">3b</a>). Moreover, in the electrolyte, the interaction between Li ions and iodine will strengthen the electron-withdrawing ability to improve the electron-deviation degree from the adjacent carbon atoms, and thus forming more electron-deficient carbon atoms. These electron-deficient carbon atoms facilitate the adsorption of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions and make iodine groups more susceptible to the nucleophilic attack by PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions and trigger the nucleophilic substitution reaction, as confirmed by the DFT calculation result showing the high binding energy of −0.35 V between I-PAN-Li<sup>+</sup> and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0003">3b</a>). During the process of nucleophilic substitution reaction, the iodine as an effective leaving group will dissociate from the I-PAN framework and dissolve in the electrolyte (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0003">3c</a>). As a result, the region with gradually reduced concentration of iodine ions is formed near the I-PAN surface. The ultraviolet and visible (UV/Vis) spectroscopy and XPS were performed to experimentally verify the nucleophilic substitution reaction and dissolution of iodine species in the electrolyte. The I-PAN powder was soaked in the carbonate electrolyte (1 M lithium hexafluorophosphate (LiPF<sub>6</sub>)/ethylene carbonate (EC)/dimethyl carbonate (DMC)/fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC), defined as blank electrolyte) or LiPF<sub>6</sub>-free solvents (EC/DMC/FEC) for the following characterizations. The UV/Vis spectrum of I-PAN soaked electrolyte (I-PAN-Electrolyte) displays three absorbance peaks at 235, 288 and 360 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which are contributed to the species of I<sup>−</sup> (235 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and I<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> (288 and 360 cm<sup>−1</sup>),<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0046_R_d151145617e688" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Energy">22</a></sup></span> confirming that iodine in I-PAN can be dissolved in the electrolyte (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0003">3d</a>). In striking contrast, these peaks are not observed in I-PAN soaked LiPF<sub>6</sub>-free solvents, which demonstrates iodine groups are not able to detach from I-PAN without LiPF<sub>6</sub>, providing a strong evidence that the dissolution of iodine is driven by the nucleophilic substitution reaction of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion. Calculated from oxygen bomb combustion-ion chromatography measurement, around 3.9 % of iodine in I-PAN can be dissolved after soaking in the blank electrolyte for 48 h. The soaked I-PAN powder was further characterized by XPS. The obvious peaks of C−P bond<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0047_R_d151145617e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Sci.">23</a></sup></span> (286.4 eV) in C 1s XPS spectrum (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0003">3e</a>) and P−C bond<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0048_R_d151145617e708" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span> (133.8 eV) in P 2p XPS spectrum (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0003">3f</a>) verify the presence of -PF<sub>6</sub> groups on I-PAN, attributed to the nucleophilic substitution reaction of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion to form the C−P bond.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202315564-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dd7c293d-fc0f-4022-9a76-6e31a15851f0/anie202315564-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/dd7c293d-fc0f-4022-9a76-6e31a15851f0/anie202315564-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/795789d7-4542-4204-be27-4c1bc55665a7/anie202315564-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dd7c293d-fc0f-4022-9a76-6e31a15851f0/anie202315564-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202315564-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202315564" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Characterization of surface properties of I-PAN. a, The surface potential of I-PAN and p-PAN detected with KPFM. b, The binding geometries and energies of Li ions with I-PAN and p-PAN. c, The nucleophilic substitution reaction between I-PAN and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>. d, UV/Vis spectra of I-PAN soaked electrolytes and LiPF<sub>6</sub>-free solvents. e, f, C 1s (e) and P 2p (f) XPS spectra of soaked I-PAN powder.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The unique surface properties of I-PAN and dissolution of iodine ions in the electrolyte significantly change the Li-ion solvation structure and desolvation behavior. Especially at the interface of I-PAN electrode and electrolyte, the strong interactions between iodine groups and Li ions weaken the coordination of Li ions and solvents, thereby enabling an iodine-assisted Li-ion desolvation effect to accelerate the Li-ion desolvation process. Raman and NMR measurements were performed to experimentally verify the iodine-assisted Li-ion desolvation effect of I-PAN. I-PAN or p-PAN powder was firstly wetted by two drops of the blank electrolyte, Raman tests were then operated at the interface of powders and the electrolyte (Figure S7). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0004">4a</a>–<a href="#anie202315564-fig-0004">b</a>, Raman spectrum of the blank electrolyte shows a series of peaks at around 715, 718–728, 742, 895, 903 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which can be assigned to EC skeletal stretching, solvated EC skeletal stretching, A<sub>1g</sub> vibration of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion, EC skeletal breathing and solvated EC skeletal breathing, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0049_R_d151145617e766" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">25</a></sup></span> When I-PAN powder was wetted by the electrolyte, the ratio of solvated EC remarkably declines, and the content of free EC increases, implying the weakened interactions between Li ions and EC solvent. Additionally, the P−F peak of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion shifts to a lower wavenumber, which indicates that PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions move further away from Li ions,<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0050_R_d151145617e775" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Commun.">26</a></sup></span> probably owing to the occurrence of nucleophilic attack of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> upon C−I at the electrolyte/electrode interface. These results demonstrate the presence of iodine group on I-PAN can enable weakened Li-ion solvation structure and promoted Li-ion desolvation of the electrolyte. NMR was further conducted to investigate the solvation chemistry of the electrolyte after adding the same amount of p-PAN or I-PAN powder. The <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectrum of the blank electrolyte displays two peaks at near 4.0 and 4.83 ppm, corresponding to the CH<sub>3</sub> of DMC and CH<sub>2</sub> of EC<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0051_R_d151145617e788" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">27</a></sup></span>, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0004">4c</a>). All of these peaks in I-PAN-Electrolyte undergo obvious up-field shifts, attributed to the reduced interactions between Li ions and solvents.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0052_R_d151145617e795" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Energy Lett.">28</a></sup></span> <sup>19</sup>F NMR spectra further confirm the weakened Li<sup>+</sup>−PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> coordination after adding I-PAN, verified by the down-field shift of the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> peak (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0004">4d</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0053_R_d151145617e812" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater.">29</a></sup></span> In contrast, the Raman and NMR peaks of p-PAN soaked electrolyte (p-PAN-Electrolyte) do not show obvious changes, further indicating p-PAN has no capability to regulate the Li-ion solvation structure of the electrolyte. Molecular dynamics (MD) simulations were further conducted to theoretically investigate the solvation structure of the electrolyte after the dissolution of iodine ions (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0004">4e</a>). In the blank electrolyte, Li ions tend to coordinate with DMC/EC solvent molecules in the first solvation shell (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0004">4f</a>). When iodine ions were dissolved in the electrolyte, they will enter the first solvation shell to form Li<sup>+</sup>−I<sup>−</sup> coordination structure with a coordination distance of 2.3 Å owing to the strong interaction between Li ions and iodine ions, which can decrease the interactions between Li<sup>+</sup>-solvent/PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and promote the desolvation process of the electrolyte, verified by the significantly reduced coordination number (CN) values of Li<sup>+</sup>-solvents (EC+DMC+FEC) and Li<sup>+</sup>−PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0004">4g</a>–<a href="#anie202315564-fig-0004">h</a>).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202315564-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/22290942-3c55-4ca3-9545-7d13802c9d5e/anie202315564-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/22290942-3c55-4ca3-9545-7d13802c9d5e/anie202315564-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a99165d7-8412-4954-96f9-3b9f6bf99e47/anie202315564-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/22290942-3c55-4ca3-9545-7d13802c9d5e/anie202315564-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202315564-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202315564" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Changed Li-ion solvation structure and promoted desolvation process by I-PAN. a, b, Raman spectra at the interface of anode materials and electrolyte. c, d, <sup>1</sup>H and <sup>19</sup>F NMR spectra of the electrolyte after soaking various anode materials. e, Snapshots of the MD simulation cells of blank electrolyte and electrolyte with I<sup>−</sup>. f, g, g(r) and CN of the blank electrolyte (f) and electrolyte with I<sup>−</sup> (g). h, Representative configurations of the solvation structure of the electrolytes with/without iodine ions.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Besides the regulation of the Li-ion solvation structure, the dissolved iodine ions from I-PAN can eliminate the Li dendrites and refresh the dead Li, which provides a way to reuse the plated Li on the anodes during extremely fast-charging process and greatly improve the fast-charging performance and battery safety. The reactivation of dead Li was investigated by placing cycled Cu electrodes with plated/dead Li into the blank electrolyte or I-PAN-Electrolyte. The cycled Cu electrodes were obtained from the disassembled Li‖Cu asymmetric cells. SEM image shows the surface of cycled Cu electrode is covered by the Li dendrites and dead Li (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0005">5a</a>). When the cycled Cu electrode is soaked in the I-PAN-Electrolyte for 1 h, the messy dendritic morphology on the Cu surface disappears, only leaving the sleek spherical SEI layer with homogeneous distribution of I and F elements, which implies Li dendrites and dead Li are successfully removed (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0005">5b</a>). Some hollow spherical morphologies are also observed, which can be attributed to the dissolution of the dead Li wrapped by the SEI layer. Additionally, after soaking the cycled Cu electrode, the I-PAN-Electrolyte changes from transparent to feculent, further suggesting the reactivation of the deposited Li (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0005">5c</a>). In contrast, when the cycled Cu electrode is soaked in the blank electrolyte for 24 h, no obvious color changes are detected (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0005">5d</a>). SEM images also show the negligible morphology changes of the cycled Cu electrode (Figure S8), indicating the blank electrolyte is powerless to reuse the dead Li. UV/Vis spectra were performed to further confirm the reactions between iodine ions and plated/dead Li (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0005">5e</a>). As mentioned above, the I-PAN-Electrolyte displays typical absorbance peaks of I<sup>−</sup> (≈235 nm) and I<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> (≈288 and 360 nm) species. After adding the cycled Cu electrode in the I-PAN-Electrolyte, the UV/Vis spectrum exhibits significantly weakened I<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> signals and enhanced I<sup>−</sup> signal. It can be speculated that the I<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> species reactivate the plated/dead Li according to the following equation (<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202315564:anie202315564-math-0001" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/a369cedf-9a1a-491b-bbbd-387561b8daca/anie202315564-math-0001.png">
).<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0046_R_d151145617e911" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Energy">22</a>, <a href="#anie202315564-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0054_R_d151145617e911" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">30</a></sup></span> When further adding the delithiated NCM811 electrode into the above electrolyte, the peaks of I<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> increase while the peak of I<sup>−</sup> weakens, indicating the delithiated NCM811 electrode can oxidize the I<sup>−</sup> to I<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> (<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202315564:anie202315564-math-0002" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/e5985034-499c-41cc-b544-f91c619ef06d/anie202315564-math-0002.png">
            <img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202315564:anie202315564-math-0003" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/b7819358-6b07-4ba5-b931-ca9e1fd47a3b/anie202315564-math-0003.png">
). Therefore, in the full cells, the I<sup>−</sup> diffused in the electrolyte can be oxidized to I<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> at cathode side, which can further shuttle to anodes to reuse the inactive Li via reversible iodine redox reactions.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202315564-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a2f40cc0-b012-4231-bcf5-85f0c9e0ca42/anie202315564-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a2f40cc0-b012-4231-bcf5-85f0c9e0ca42/anie202315564-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cc1b9b51-d183-4210-bbbf-884cb4f83d3e/anie202315564-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a2f40cc0-b012-4231-bcf5-85f0c9e0ca42/anie202315564-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202315564-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202315564" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Reutilization of dead Li, regulated EDL structure, and bulk Li-ion transport kinetics of I-PAN. a, b, SEM images of cycled Cu electrode with plated/dead Li before (a) and after (b) soaking with I-PAN-Electrolyte for 1 h. c, d, Digital images of electrolytes and cycled Cu electrodes at various soaking times in I-PAN-Electrolyte (c) and blank electrolyte (d). e, UV/Vis spectra of I-PAN-Electrolyte, I-PAN-Electrolyte with dead Li, and I-PAN-Electrolyte with dead Li and further delithiated NCM811. f, Schematic illustration of the EDL on p-PAN and I-PAN electrode surfaces. g, Zeta potentials of I-PAN and p-PAN in the carbonate electrolyte. h, EIS curves of anodes after 5 cycles (R<sub>0</sub>-ohmic resistance, R<sub>sf</sub>-surface film resistance, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>-charge transfer resistance, CPE-constant phase element). i, j, The simulated Li-ion diffusion path and energy barriers on I-PAN and p-PAN.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The rich iodine groups on I-PAN also alter the EDL structure covered on the I-PAN surface in the electrolyte. Based on the equation of <img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202315564:anie202315564-math-0004" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/1d387bdf-0159-472b-b02f-f4c50753b8b2/anie202315564-math-0004.png">
, where <i>δ</i><sub>r</sub>, <i>k</i> and <i>σ</i> are the thickness of EDL, Debye–Hückel length and surface charge density, respectively,<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0055_R_d151145617e977" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">31</a></sup></span> the thickness of EDL can be tuned by regulating the surface charge density of electrode materials. I-PAN with abundant iodine groups notably enhances the surface charge density due to the existence of lone pair electrons, which has been confirmed by the aforementioned surface potential measurement of KPFM (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0003">3a</a>). The increased surface charge density can enable more adsorption of Li ions and decrease the thickness of EDL.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0055_R_d151145617e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">31</a></sup></span> The thinner EDL can significantly shorten the distance for Li ions reaching the surface of I-PAN and enhance the Li-ion transport kinetics, which is beneficial for achieving high fast-charging performance. Meanwhile, the nucleophilic substitution reaction on the surface of I-PAN in the electrolyte leads to the increased iodine ions and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions, which locate at the closest surface of I-PAN to form the IHP of the EDL (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0005">5f</a>). The presence of abundant anions in the IHP leads to a negative zeta potential of −5.1 mV in the carbonate electrolyte, which is greatly different from that of p-PAN (+1.5 mV in carbonate electrolyte) (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0005">5g</a>).</p>
         
         <p>The unique surface structure and changed EDL endow the charge storage process of I-PAN with obvious capacitive characteristics (Figure S9–10). Compared with p-PAN anode, I-PAN anode exhibits improved capacitive contributions especially at high current densities (Figure S11), which is favorable for enhancing charge storage and fast-charging performance of the electrodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0056_R_d151145617e999" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32">32</a></sup></span> Based on the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) results, the calculated Li-ion diffusion coefficient of I-PAN is 2.0×10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, much higher than that of p-PAN (5.0×10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0005">5h</a>, Figure S12, Table S1). The DFT calculation demonstrates the simulated Li-ion transport pathways along in-plane directions of the p-PAN and I-PAN (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0005">5i</a>). The normalized Li-ion diffusion barrier of I-PAN is around 0.9 eV, which is smaller than that of p-PAN (1.1 eV), theoretically indicating the existence of iodine group can notably improve the intrinsic Li-ion diffusion kinetics of I-PAN (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0005">5j</a>).</p>
         
         <p>The rich iodine group, regulated Li-ion solvation structure, and changed IHP of the EDL significantly influence the formation of the SEI layer. The chemical composition and distribution of SEI throughout the cycled electrodes were analyzed using depth-profiling XPS and time-of-flight secondary-ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS). As displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0006">6a</a>, the F 1s XPS spectra of p-PAN anode exhibit four deconvoluted peaks at 684.5, 685.7, 686.8 and 691.9 eV. These peaks can be assigned to LiF, Li<sub>x</sub>PO<sub>y</sub>F<sub>z</sub>, Li<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>z</sub> and CF<sub>x</sub>, respectively, which are attributed to the decomposition of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and FEC species.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0059_R_d151145617e1047" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Energy Lett.">33</a></sup></span> As the Ar<sup>+</sup> sputtering proceeded, the signal of CF<sub>x</sub> group disappears, and the ratios of LiF gradually increase from 35 % to 55.1 %, indicating LiF is more abundant in the inner SEI layer. This SEI layer with organic outer layer and inorganic inner layer is similar to the characteristic SEI formed on the traditional anodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0028_R_d151145617e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11a - Nat. Energy">11a</a>, <a href="#anie202315564-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0060_R_d151145617e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a></sup></span> As to the I-PAN anode (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0006">6b</a>), the F 1s XPS spectra only show three typical peaks of LiF, Li<sub>x</sub>PO<sub>y</sub>F<sub>z</sub>, and Li<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>z</sub>, which are mainly originated from the decomposition of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> in IHP of the EDL. The LiF content in the SEI layer of I-PAN anodes is much higher than that of p-PAN anodes, and LiF dominates the composition throughout the entire SEI layer (57.6 % to 75 %), according with the features of anion-derived SEI. Besides LiF, LiI is present in the SEI with a higher content in the inner layer, as shown in I 3d XPS spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0006">6c</a>),<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0061_R_d151145617e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35">35</a></sup></span> which further confirms that the dissolved iodine ions from I-PAN concentrate on the surface of the anodes to generate the LiI-rich SEI. The C 1s and O 1s XPS spectra demonstrate the SEI layer on the I-PAN anodes also contains more Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> species than that of p-PAN (Figure S13–14).<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0064_R_d151145617e1086" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Commun.">36</a></sup></span> TOF-SIMS was used to further elucidate the detailed structure of the SEI. Compared with p-PAN (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0006">6d</a>), the 3D visualization images of the cycled I-PAN (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0006">6e</a>) display a strong signal of I<sup>−</sup> mass fragment, indicating the iodine-related species exist in the SEI layer. Much stronger inorganic LiF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> signal and weaker PO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>/C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup> signals further demonstrate the components of the SEI layer are mainly originated from the decomposition of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion,<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0060_R_d151145617e1112" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a></sup></span> and the decomposition of the electrolyte solvents is significantly suppressed due to the regulated Li-ion solvation structure and IHP of the EDL. TOF-SIMS depth profiles of I-PAN electrode (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0006">6f</a>) also show significantly stronger LiF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> and I<sup>−</sup> signals than other secondary ion species, indicating LiF and LiI are the main components of the SEI layer. Additionally, the I<sup>−</sup> signal gradually enhances with the increase of sputtering time, further confirming the iodine group detaches from I-PAN skeleton to participate in the formation of SEI, which is highly consistent with the XPS depth-profiling results. The HR-TEM was also performed to study the morphology of the formed SEI layers. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0006">6g</a>, the cycled I-PAN electrode clearly displays two types of lattice fringes with distances of around 2.32 Å and 3.45 Å, corresponding to the (111) planes of LiF and LiI, respectively. As a comparison, the cycled p-PAN electrode shows a mussy and amorphous SEI layer, suggesting more content of organic species (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0006">6h</a>). The above results indicate that the I-PAN anode with unique surface properties tends to generate an inorganic SEI layer rich of LiF and LiI, which have high mechanical strength/ionic conductivity and are favorable for achieving high fast-charging capability and good cycling performance (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0006">6i</a>).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202315564-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eb56ae63-8665-44b4-a32c-3aaac6bba19e/anie202315564-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/eb56ae63-8665-44b4-a32c-3aaac6bba19e/anie202315564-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e98d048a-6371-4976-8175-9c91a6901b24/anie202315564-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eb56ae63-8665-44b4-a32c-3aaac6bba19e/anie202315564-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202315564-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202315564" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Characterization of SEI layers on I-PAN and p-PAN anodes. a, b, Depth-profiling F 1s XPS spectra of p-PAN (a) and I-PAN (b). c, I 3d XPS spectrum of I-PAN. d, e, Spatial distribution of various ions on p-PAN (d) and I-PAN (e) anodes detected by TOF-SIMS. f, Depth profiles of I-PAN and p-PAN anodes. g, h, HR-TEM images of the SEI layer on I-PAN (g) and p-PAN (h) anodes after 10 cycles. i, Schematic representation of the SEI layers formed on I-PAN and p-PAN anodes.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Benefitting from the expanded ion-transport channels and unique surface properties enabled by iodine group, I-PAN anode shows significantly enhanced fast-charging performance in Li-ion and Na-ion batteries. In Li-ion batteries, the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curve of I-PAN anode displays a cathodic peak at near 0.6 V in the first cycle, which is probably attributed to the formation of SEI layer derived from PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and I<sup>−</sup> anions (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0007">7a</a>). Compared with p-PAN, I-PAN anode displays an excellent rate performance with high reversible capacities from 0.1 to 20 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0007">7b</a>, Figure S15). The I-PAN also delivers stable cycling performance at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S16), and 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a high initial capacity of 488.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and capacity retention of 85.4 % after 4000 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0007">7c</a>, Figure S17). The outstanding fast-charging capability of I-PAN anodes is reflected by the exceptional cycling performance at extremely high current densities of 10 and 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>(Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0007">7d</a>, Figure S18). At 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the I-PAN anode delivers a high discharge capacity of 325.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (that is 56 % of the capacity at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> delivered in 35 seconds) and maintains a stable cycling for 3000 cycles. Notably, even at an ultra-high current density of 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a reversible capacity of 228.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved for I-PAN anode, with a high capacity retention of 80.7 % after an ultra-long lifespan of 10000 cycles, showing overwhelming superiority over the p-PAN anodes. The increasing tendency of initial capacity at 10/20 A g<sup>−1</sup> is probably attributed to the electrochemical activation process for the batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202315564-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0028_R_d151145617e1202" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11a - Nat. Energy">11a</a>, <a href="#anie202315564-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202315564-bib-0034_R_d151145617e1202" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nature">14</a></sup></span> Note that the average working voltage of I-PAN is around 0.3 V (versus a Li/Li<sup>+</sup> reference electrode) (Figure S15–16), suitable for fabricating high-energy-density fast-charging batteries. At a low temperature of −10 °C, the I-PAN anode also shows an attractive reversible capacity and a slight capacity degradation rate (≈0.02 % per cycle at 0.1 and 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>) during 600 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0007">7e</a>, Figure S19). Compared with other advanced high-rate anode materials, I-PAN shows a superior fast-charging capability and extended cycling performance (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0007">7f</a>, Table S2). The unprecedented electrochemical performance of I-PAN anode under harsh conditions (high current densities, low temperatures) suggests that the iodine groups in I-PAN can comprehensively optimize the critical factors influencing the Li-ion transport kinetics, and show a decisive impact on eliminating the Li metal plating and mitigating the detrimental side reactions, making a qualitative leap in the fast-charging performance. The I-PAN||NCM811 full cells were assembled to further evaluate the fast-charging performance (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0007">7g</a>, Figure S20). At 5 C, the full cell exhibits high reversible capacities of 156.9 and 133.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1<sup>st</sup> and 1000<sup>th</sup> cycles, respectively. Under an extremely fast-charging condition of 10 C (charging time: ≈5 min), the full cell still delivers an initial capacity of 112.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and excellent capacity retention of 91.4 % after 1000 cycles. Such excellent fast-charging battery performance has never been reported for other advanced anode materials (Table S2).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202315564-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9196b9b0-3154-4555-8127-c4d8e00a0c24/anie202315564-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9196b9b0-3154-4555-8127-c4d8e00a0c24/anie202315564-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2a0f49d2-f00d-43d9-9acf-b297d37babf9/anie202315564-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9196b9b0-3154-4555-8127-c4d8e00a0c24/anie202315564-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202315564-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202315564" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical performance of I-PAN anodes. a, CV curves of I-PAN and p-PAN anodes. b, Rate performance of I-PAN and p-PAN anodes. c, Cycling performance of the anodes at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. d, Long-term cycling performance at extremely high current densities. e, Cycling performance of I-PAN anodes at −10 °C. f, Comparison of fast-charging performance of I-PAN with other advanced fast-charging anode materials. g, Extremely fast-charging performance of I-PAN||NCM811 full cells. h, Electrochemical performance of I-PAN anodes in Na-ion batteries at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>We further investigated the electrochemical performance of I-PAN anodes in Na-ion batteries. The I-PAN anode shows much better rate capability (Figure S21) and cycling performance (Figure S22) than p-PAN. At 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the I-PAN anode displays a high reversible capacity of 210 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and negligible capacity degradation within 2000 cycles (Figure S23). Even at a high current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, an impressive initial capacity of 186.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved with a capacity retention of 87.2 % after 3000 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202315564-fig-0007">7h</a>, Figure S24). These results indicate the I-PAN anode also delivers excellent fast-charging performance and shows great promise in advanced fast-charging Na-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202315564-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202315564-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We reported a I-PAN anode for fast-charging Li-ion and Na-ion batteries. I-PAN features expanded ion transport channels, iodine-assisted Li-ion desolvation effect, and regulated EDL structure. The released iodine ions from I-PAN through nucleophilic substitution reaction of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> change the Li-ion solvation structure and further promote the desolvation process. The plated/dead Li formed on the anodes under fast-charging conditions can be reused through the reversible I<sup>−</sup>/I<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> redox reactions to compensate the Li loss. Thus, I-PAN can enable fast bulk/interphase Li-ion diffusion kinetics, accelerated desolvation process, formation of LiF/LiI-rich SEI layer, and reutilization of dead Li. I-PAN anode shows unprecedent fast-charging performance with a high capacity and ultra-long cycling life at a high current density of 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The I-PAN||NCM811 full cell delivers an attractive capacity of 112.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and retention of 91.4 % after 1000 cycles at an extremely high rate of 10 C. The high-performance fast-charging Na-ion batteries using I-PAN anode are also demonstrated. Our work provides a promising fast-charging anode architecture and opens an avenue to develop high-quality fast-charging anode materials with practical applications in high-performance fast-charging batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202315564-sec-0004-title"> Acknowledgments</h2>
